## Chapter: Fundamentals of Photonics: Quantum Electronics

### Introduction



In this chapter, we will explore the quantum nature of light and matter. This is a fundamental concept in the field of photonics, as it helps us understand the behavior of light and matter at the smallest scales. We will delve into the principles of quantum mechanics and how they apply to the interactions between light and matter. This will include topics such as wave-particle duality, quantization of energy, and the role of photons in the quantum world.



One of the key concepts we will cover is the wave-particle duality of light. This idea suggests that light can behave as both a wave and a particle, depending on the context. This may seem counterintuitive, but it has been proven through various experiments and has significant implications for our understanding of light and matter. We will also discuss how this duality applies to other particles, such as electrons, and how it relates to the concept of energy quantization.



Speaking of energy quantization, we will also explore how this concept plays a crucial role in the interactions between light and matter. In the quantum world, energy is not continuous but rather exists in discrete packets called quanta. This has significant implications for the behavior of light and matter, and we will discuss how it affects phenomena such as absorption, emission, and scattering.



Finally, we will delve into the role of photons in the quantum world. Photons are the fundamental particles of light, and they play a crucial role in the interactions between light and matter. We will discuss their properties, such as energy and momentum, and how they are involved in processes such as absorption and emission of light.



Overall, this chapter will provide a solid foundation for understanding the quantum nature of light and matter in the field of photonics. By the end, you will have a better understanding of the fundamental principles that govern the behavior of light and matter at the smallest scales. 





## Chapter 4: Quantum Nature of Light and Matter



### Section: 4.1 Quantum Nature of Light and Matter (Review)



In this section, we will review the fundamental principles of quantum mechanics and how they apply to the interactions between light and matter. This will provide a solid foundation for understanding the quantum nature of light and matter in the field of photonics.



#### 4.1a Introduction to Quantum Nature of Light and Matter (Review)



Quantum mechanics is a branch of physics that describes the behavior of particles at the smallest scales. It is a highly successful theory that has been confirmed by numerous experiments and has revolutionized our understanding of the physical world. However, it also raises questions about the underlying nature of reality, which has led to various interpretations of quantum mechanics.



One of the key concepts in quantum mechanics is the wave-particle duality of light. This idea suggests that light can behave as both a wave and a particle, depending on the context. This may seem counterintuitive, but it has been proven through various experiments, such as the double-slit experiment, where light behaves as both a wave and a particle simultaneously. This duality also applies to other particles, such as electrons, and has significant implications for our understanding of energy quantization.



Energy quantization is another fundamental concept in quantum mechanics. It states that energy is not continuous but rather exists in discrete packets called quanta. This has significant implications for the behavior of light and matter, as it explains phenomena such as the discrete energy levels of atoms and the quantization of light emission and absorption.



The role of photons in the quantum world is also crucial. Photons are the fundamental particles of light, and they play a crucial role in the interactions between light and matter. They have properties such as energy and momentum, and their quantized nature explains the discrete energy levels of atoms and the quantization of light emission and absorption.



In summary, the principles of quantum mechanics, such as wave-particle duality and energy quantization, are essential for understanding the quantum nature of light and matter. In the next sections, we will delve deeper into these concepts and explore their applications in the field of photonics. 





# Fundamentals of Photonics: Quantum Electronics



## Chapter 4: Quantum Nature of Light and Matter



### Section: 4.1 Quantum Nature of Light and Matter (Review)



In this section, we will review the fundamental principles of quantum mechanics and how they apply to the interactions between light and matter. This will provide a solid foundation for understanding the quantum nature of light and matter in the field of photonics.



#### 4.1a Introduction to Quantum Nature of Light and Matter (Review)



Quantum mechanics is a branch of physics that describes the behavior of particles at the smallest scales. It is a highly successful theory that has been confirmed by numerous experiments and has revolutionized our understanding of the physical world. However, it also raises questions about the underlying nature of reality, which has led to various interpretations of quantum mechanics.



One of the key concepts in quantum mechanics is the wave-particle duality of light. This idea suggests that light can behave as both a wave and a particle, depending on the context. This may seem counterintuitive, but it has been proven through various experiments, such as the double-slit experiment, where light behaves as both a wave and a particle simultaneously. This duality also applies to other particles, such as electrons, and has significant implications for our understanding of energy quantization.



Energy quantization is another fundamental concept in quantum mechanics. It states that energy is not continuous but rather exists in discrete packets called quanta. This has significant implications for the behavior of light and matter, as it explains phenomena such as the discrete energy levels of atoms and the quantization of light emission and absorption.



The role of photons in the quantum world is also crucial. Photons are the fundamental particles of light, and they play a crucial role in the interactions between light and matter. They have properties such as energy and momentum, and their quantized nature explains the discrete energy levels of atoms and the quantization of light emission and absorption.



### Subsection: 4.1b Wave-Particle Duality and Uncertainty Principle



The wave-particle duality of light is a fundamental concept in quantum mechanics that describes the dual nature of light as both a wave and a particle. This duality is not limited to light, but also applies to other particles, such as electrons. It is a key principle in understanding the behavior of light and matter in the field of photonics.



The wave-like behavior of light can be observed through phenomena such as diffraction and interference. When a photon is detected by a measuring instrument, it is registered as a single, particulate unit. However, the probability of detecting a photon is calculated using equations that describe waves. This combination of wave-like and particle-like behavior is known as wave-particle duality.



The uncertainty principle, also known as Heisenberg's uncertainty principle, is another fundamental concept in quantum mechanics. It states that it is impossible to know both the position and momentum of a particle with absolute certainty. This principle applies to all particles, including photons, and has significant implications for the behavior of light and matter.



The uncertainty principle is closely related to the wave-particle duality of light. The more accurately we know the position of a particle, the less accurately we can know its momentum, and vice versa. This is because the act of measuring one property of a particle affects the other property. This principle has been confirmed through various experiments, such as the double-slit experiment, and is a crucial aspect of understanding the quantum nature of light and matter.



In conclusion, the wave-particle duality and uncertainty principle are fundamental concepts in quantum mechanics that play a crucial role in understanding the behavior of light and matter in the field of photonics. These principles have been confirmed through numerous experiments and have revolutionized our understanding of the physical world. In the next section, we will explore the second-quantized theory of photons, known as quantum electrodynamics, and its role in understanding the quantum nature of light and matter.





# Fundamentals of Photonics: Quantum Electronics



## Chapter 4: Quantum Nature of Light and Matter



### Section: 4.1 Quantum Nature of Light and Matter (Review)



In this section, we will review the fundamental principles of quantum mechanics and how they apply to the interactions between light and matter. This will provide a solid foundation for understanding the quantum nature of light and matter in the field of photonics.



#### 4.1a Introduction to Quantum Nature of Light and Matter (Review)



Quantum mechanics is a branch of physics that describes the behavior of particles at the smallest scales. It is a highly successful theory that has been confirmed by numerous experiments and has revolutionized our understanding of the physical world. However, it also raises questions about the underlying nature of reality, which has led to various interpretations of quantum mechanics.



One of the key concepts in quantum mechanics is the wave-particle duality of light. This idea suggests that light can behave as both a wave and a particle, depending on the context. This may seem counterintuitive, but it has been proven through various experiments, such as the double-slit experiment, where light behaves as both a wave and a particle simultaneously. This duality also applies to other particles, such as electrons, and has significant implications for our understanding of energy quantization.



Energy quantization is another fundamental concept in quantum mechanics. It states that energy is not continuous but rather exists in discrete packets called quanta. This has significant implications for the behavior of light and matter, as it explains phenomena such as the discrete energy levels of atoms and the quantization of light emission and absorption.



The role of photons in the quantum world is also crucial. Photons are the fundamental particles of light, and they play a crucial role in the interactions between light and matter. They have properties such as energy and momentum, and their behavior is described by the laws of quantum mechanics. In the context of photonics, understanding the quantum nature of light is essential for designing and manipulating devices such as lasers and detectors.



#### 4.1b Quantum Superposition and Entanglement



Another important concept in quantum mechanics is superposition, which refers to the ability of a quantum system to exist in multiple states simultaneously. This is in contrast to classical systems, where a system can only exist in one state at a time. Superposition is a fundamental principle that allows for the creation of quantum computers and other advanced technologies.



Entanglement is another phenomenon that arises from the principles of quantum mechanics. It occurs when two or more particles become correlated in such a way that the state of one particle cannot be described without considering the state of the other particle. This phenomenon has been demonstrated through experiments and has potential applications in quantum communication and cryptography.



#### 4.1c Quantum Superposition and Interference



Quantum interference is a consequence of the wave-like nature of particles in the quantum world. It occurs when two or more quantum states interfere with each other, resulting in a new state that is a combination of the original states. This interference can be constructive, where the amplitudes of the states add up, or destructive, where the amplitudes cancel each other out.



In the context of photonics, quantum interference plays a crucial role in the behavior of light. For example, in the double-slit experiment, the interference of light waves results in the characteristic interference pattern on the screen. This phenomenon is also utilized in devices such as interferometers, which are used for precise measurements in fields such as astronomy and telecommunications.



In conclusion, the quantum nature of light and matter is a fundamental aspect of photonics. Understanding the principles of quantum mechanics and how they apply to the interactions between light and matter is essential for developing advanced technologies and furthering our understanding of the physical world. In the next section, we will delve deeper into the quantum nature of light and matter and explore its implications in the field of photonics.





# Fundamentals of Photonics: Quantum Electronics



## Chapter 4: Quantum Nature of Light and Matter



### Section: 4.2 Schrödinger Equation and Stationary States (Review)



In the previous section, we discussed the fundamental principles of quantum mechanics and how they apply to the interactions between light and matter. In this section, we will delve deeper into the mathematical framework of quantum mechanics and explore the Schrödinger equation and stationary states.



#### 4.2a Introduction to Schrödinger Equation and Stationary States (Review)



The Schrödinger equation is a fundamental equation in quantum mechanics that describes the time evolution of a quantum system. It was first proposed by Erwin Schrödinger in 1926 and has since become a cornerstone of quantum mechanics.



The Schrödinger equation is given by:


$$

i\hbar\frac{\partial}{\partial t}\psi(\mathbf{r},t) = \hat{H}\psi(\mathbf{r},t)

$$


where $\psi(\mathbf{r},t)$ is the wave function of the system, $\hat{H}$ is the Hamiltonian operator, and $\hbar$ is the reduced Planck's constant.



One of the key features of the Schrödinger equation is that it allows us to calculate the probability of finding a particle in a particular state. This is given by the square of the wave function, $|\psi(\mathbf{r},t)|^2$, which represents the probability density of finding the particle at a particular position.



In the case of stationary states, the wave function does not change with time, and the Schrödinger equation reduces to a simpler form:


$$

\hat{H}\psi(\mathbf{r}) = E\psi(\mathbf{r})

$$


where $E$ is the energy of the system. This equation is known as the time-independent Schrödinger equation and is used to calculate the energy eigenvalues and eigenfunctions of a quantum system.



For periodic solids with one atom per unit cell, the calculation of stationary states is simplified considerably. This is because all of the potentials, $v_i(\mathbf{r}_i)$, are the same, and the nuclear positions, $\mathbf{R}_i$, form a periodic array. This allows us to use Bloch's theorem, which states that the solutions of the Schrödinger equation can be written as a Bloch wave:


$$

\psi_{\mathbf{k}}(\mathbf{r}+\mathbf{R}_i) = e^{i\mathbf{k}\cdot\mathbf{R}_i}\psi_{\mathbf{k}}(\mathbf{r})

$$


where $\mathbf{k}$ is the wave vector.



To simplify the calculation further, we can use a symmetric matrix for the coefficients, which can be achieved by defining:


$$

c_{l'm'}^j = e^{-i\mathbf{k}\cdot\mathbf{R}_j}c_{l'm'}(E,\mathbf{k})

$$


These coefficients satisfy a set of linear equations:


$$

\sum_{j,l'm'}M_{lm,l'm'}^{ij}c_{l'm'}^j = 0

$$


where the elements of the matrix $\mathbf{M}$ are given by:


$$

M_{lm,l'm'}^{ij} = m_{lm,l'm'}^i - A_{lm,l'm'}(E,\mathbf{k})

$$


and the $m_{lm,l'm'}^i$ are the elements of the inverse of the t-matrix.



The coefficients $c_{l'm'}(E,\mathbf{k})$ satisfy the homogeneous equations:


$$

\sum_{l'm'}M_{lm,l'm'}(E,\mathbf{k})c_{l'm'}(E,\mathbf{k}) = 0

$$


where $M_{lm,l'm'}(E,\mathbf{k}) = m_{lm,l'm'}(E) - A_{lm,l'm'}(E,\mathbf{k})$ and $A_{lm,l'm'}(E,\mathbf{k}) = \sum_j e^{i\mathbf{k}\cdot\mathbf{R}_{ij}}g_{lm,l'm'}(E,\mathbf{R}_{ij})$.



This method, known as the Korringa-Kohn-Rostoker method (KKR method), was first derived by Walter Kohn and Norman Rostoker using the Kohn variational method. It has become a popular approach for band theory calculations in periodic solids.



In conclusion, the Schrödinger equation and stationary states play a crucial role in understanding the quantum nature of light and matter. They provide a mathematical framework for calculating the behavior of quantum systems and have been instrumental in advancing our understanding of the physical world. 





# Fundamentals of Photonics: Quantum Electronics



## Chapter 4: Quantum Nature of Light and Matter



### Section: 4.2 Schrödinger Equation and Stationary States (Review)



In the previous section, we discussed the fundamental principles of quantum mechanics and how they apply to the interactions between light and matter. In this section, we will delve deeper into the mathematical framework of quantum mechanics and explore the Schrödinger equation and stationary states.



#### 4.2a Introduction to Schrödinger Equation and Stationary States (Review)



The Schrödinger equation is a fundamental equation in quantum mechanics that describes the time evolution of a quantum system. It was first proposed by Erwin Schrödinger in 1926 and has since become a cornerstone of quantum mechanics.



The Schrödinger equation is given by:


$$

i\hbar\frac{\partial}{\partial t}\psi(\mathbf{r},t) = \hat{H}\psi(\mathbf{r},t)

$$


where $\psi(\mathbf{r},t)$ is the wave function of the system, $\hat{H}$ is the Hamiltonian operator, and $\hbar$ is the reduced Planck's constant.



One of the key features of the Schrödinger equation is that it allows us to calculate the probability of finding a particle in a particular state. This is given by the square of the wave function, $|\psi(\mathbf{r},t)|^2$, which represents the probability density of finding the particle at a particular position.



In the case of stationary states, the wave function does not change with time, and the Schrödinger equation reduces to a simpler form:


$$

\hat{H}\psi(\mathbf{r}) = E\psi(\mathbf{r})

$$


where $E$ is the energy of the system. This equation is known as the time-independent Schrödinger equation and is used to calculate the energy eigenvalues and eigenfunctions of a quantum system.



For periodic solids with one atom per unit cell, the calculation of stationary states is simplified considerably. This is because all of the potentials, $v_i(\mathbf{r}_i)$, are the same, and the nuclear positions, $\mathbf{R}_i$, form a regular lattice. In this case, the Hamiltonian operator can be written as:


$$

\hat{H} = \sum_{i=1}^{N} \frac{\hat{p}_i^2}{2m} + \sum_{i=1}^{N} v(\mathbf{r}_i)

$$


where $\hat{p}_i$ is the momentum operator for the $i$th particle and $v(\mathbf{r}_i)$ is the potential energy at the position of the $i$th particle. This simplification allows us to solve the time-independent Schrödinger equation using the Bloch theorem, which states that the wave function can be written as a product of a periodic function and a plane wave:


$$

\psi(\mathbf{r}) = u(\mathbf{r})e^{i\mathbf{k}\cdot\mathbf{r}}

$$


where $u(\mathbf{r})$ is the periodic function and $\mathbf{k}$ is the wave vector. This leads to the concept of energy bands and band structure in solids, which will be discussed in more detail in later chapters.



In addition to stationary states, the Schrödinger equation also allows us to calculate the time evolution of a quantum system. This is done using the time evolution operator, $\mathbf{U}(t)$, which is defined as:


$$

\mathbf{U}(t) = e^{-i\mathbf{H}t/\hbar}

$$


This operator describes how the wave function evolves over time and is unitary, meaning that $\mathbf{U}^\dagger \mathbf{U} = 1$. The time evolution operator can also be written in terms of the Hamiltonian and the time evolution matrix, $\mathbf{U}(t) = \mathbf{U}(t) \mathbf{c}_0$, where $\mathbf{c}_0$ is the state vector at $t=0$.



In conclusion, the Schrödinger equation and stationary states play a crucial role in understanding the quantum nature of light and matter. They allow us to calculate the probability of finding a particle in a particular state and the time evolution of a quantum system. In the next section, we will explore the concept of quantum superposition and its implications in quantum electronics.





# Fundamentals of Photonics: Quantum Electronics



## Chapter 4: Quantum Nature of Light and Matter



### Section: 4.2 Schrödinger Equation and Stationary States (Review)



In the previous section, we discussed the fundamental principles of quantum mechanics and how they apply to the interactions between light and matter. In this section, we will delve deeper into the mathematical framework of quantum mechanics and explore the Schrödinger equation and stationary states.



#### 4.2a Introduction to Schrödinger Equation and Stationary States (Review)



The Schrödinger equation is a fundamental equation in quantum mechanics that describes the time evolution of a quantum system. It was first proposed by Erwin Schrödinger in 1926 and has since become a cornerstone of quantum mechanics.



The Schrödinger equation is given by:


$$

i\hbar\frac{\partial}{\partial t}\psi(\mathbf{r},t) = \hat{H}\psi(\mathbf{r},t)

$$


where $\psi(\mathbf{r},t)$ is the wave function of the system, $\hat{H}$ is the Hamiltonian operator, and $\hbar$ is the reduced Planck's constant.



One of the key features of the Schrödinger equation is that it allows us to calculate the probability of finding a particle in a particular state. This is given by the square of the wave function, $|\psi(\mathbf{r},t)|^2$, which represents the probability density of finding the particle at a particular position.



In the case of stationary states, the wave function does not change with time, and the Schrödinger equation reduces to a simpler form:


$$

\hat{H}\psi(\mathbf{r}) = E\psi(\mathbf{r})

$$


where $E$ is the energy of the system. This equation is known as the time-independent Schrödinger equation and is used to calculate the energy eigenvalues and eigenfunctions of a quantum system.



In this section, we will review the concept of stationary states and their importance in understanding the quantum nature of light and matter. We will also explore the quantum harmonic oscillator and the hydrogen atom as examples of stationary states in quantum mechanics.



#### 4.2b Quantum Harmonic Oscillator



The quantum harmonic oscillator is a fundamental model in quantum mechanics that describes the behavior of a particle in a potential well. It is a simple system that can be solved analytically, making it a useful tool for understanding the principles of quantum mechanics.



In the quantum harmonic oscillator, the potential energy is given by:


$$

V(x) = \frac{1}{2}m\omega^2x^2

$$


where $m$ is the mass of the particle and $\omega$ is the angular frequency of the oscillator. The Hamiltonian operator for this system is given by:


$$

\hat{H} = \frac{\hat{p}^2}{2m} + \frac{1}{2}m\omega^2\hat{x}^2

$$


where $\hat{p}$ is the momentum operator and $\hat{x}$ is the position operator.



Using the time-independent Schrödinger equation, we can solve for the energy eigenvalues and eigenfunctions of the quantum harmonic oscillator. The energy eigenvalues are given by:


$$

E_n = \left(n+\frac{1}{2}\right)\hbar\omega

$$


where $n$ is a non-negative integer. The corresponding eigenfunctions are given by:


$$

\psi_n(x) = \left(\frac{m\omega}{\pi\hbar}\right)^{1/4}\frac{1}{\sqrt{2^n n!}}H_n\left(\sqrt{\frac{m\omega}{\hbar}}x\right)e^{-\frac{m\omega}{2\hbar}x^2}

$$


where $H_n(x)$ is the Hermite polynomial of degree $n$.



The quantum harmonic oscillator has many applications in physics, including in the study of molecular vibrations and the behavior of electrons in atoms.



#### 4.2c Hydrogen Atom



The hydrogen atom is another important example of a stationary state in quantum mechanics. It consists of a single proton (nucleus) and a single electron orbiting around it. The potential energy of the hydrogen atom is given by:


$$

V(r) = -\frac{e^2}{4\pi\epsilon_0 r}

$$


where $e$ is the elementary charge, $\epsilon_0$ is the permittivity of free space, and $r$ is the distance between the proton and the electron.



Using the time-independent Schrödinger equation, we can solve for the energy eigenvalues and eigenfunctions of the hydrogen atom. The energy eigenvalues are given by:


$$

E_n = -\frac{me^4}{8\pi^2\epsilon_0^2\hbar^2n^2}

$$


where $n$ is a positive integer known as the principal quantum number. The corresponding eigenfunctions are given by:


$$

\psi_{nlm}(r,\theta,\phi) = R_{nl}(r)Y_{lm}(\theta,\phi)

$$


where $R_{nl}(r)$ is the radial wave function and $Y_{lm}(\theta,\phi)$ is the spherical harmonic function.



The hydrogen atom is a crucial system in understanding the principles of quantum mechanics and has many applications in atomic and molecular physics.



In the next section, we will explore the concept of quantum entanglement and its implications in quantum electronics. We will also discuss the product operator formalism and its applications in understanding the interactions between light and matter.





# Fundamentals of Photonics: Quantum Electronics



## Chapter 4: Quantum Nature of Light and Matter



### Section: 4.3 Harmonic Oscillator and Hydrogen Atom



### Subsection: 4.3a Introduction to Harmonic Oscillator and Hydrogen Atom



In the previous section, we discussed the concept of stationary states and their importance in understanding the quantum nature of light and matter. In this section, we will explore two important examples of stationary states: the quantum harmonic oscillator and the hydrogen atom.



#### The Quantum Harmonic Oscillator



The quantum harmonic oscillator is a model that describes the behavior of a particle in a potential well that is proportional to the square of its displacement from the equilibrium position. This model is important in understanding the behavior of atoms, molecules, and other systems in quantum mechanics.



The Hamiltonian operator for the quantum harmonic oscillator is given by:


$$

\hat{H} = \frac{\hat{p}^2}{2m} + \frac{1}{2}m\omega^2\hat{x}^2

$$


where $\hat{p}$ is the momentum operator, $m$ is the mass of the particle, $\omega$ is the angular frequency of the oscillator, and $\hat{x}$ is the position operator.



Using the time-independent Schrödinger equation, we can solve for the energy eigenvalues and eigenfunctions of the quantum harmonic oscillator. The energy eigenvalues are given by:


$$

E_n = \left(n+\frac{1}{2}\right)\hbar\omega

$$


where $n$ is a non-negative integer. The corresponding eigenfunctions are given by:


$$

\psi_n(x) = \left(\frac{m\omega}{\pi\hbar}\right)^{\frac{1}{4}}\frac{1}{\sqrt{2^n n!}}H_n\left(\sqrt{\frac{m\omega}{\hbar}}x\right)e^{-\frac{m\omega x^2}{2\hbar}}

$$


where $H_n(x)$ is the Hermite polynomial of degree $n$.



The quantum harmonic oscillator has many applications in physics, including in the study of molecular vibrations, phonons in solids, and the behavior of electrons in atoms.



#### The Hydrogen Atom



The hydrogen atom is the simplest example of a system with a single electron bound to a nucleus. It is also one of the most important systems in quantum mechanics, as it provides a basis for understanding the behavior of more complex atoms and molecules.



The Hamiltonian operator for the hydrogen atom is given by:


$$

\hat{H} = -\frac{\hbar^2}{2m_e}\nabla^2 - \frac{e^2}{4\pi\epsilon_0r}

$$


where $m_e$ is the mass of the electron, $e$ is the elementary charge, $\epsilon_0$ is the permittivity of free space, and $r$ is the distance between the electron and the nucleus.



Using the time-independent Schrödinger equation, we can solve for the energy eigenvalues and eigenfunctions of the hydrogen atom. The energy eigenvalues are given by:


$$

E_n = -\frac{me^4}{32\pi^2\epsilon_0^2\hbar^2n^2}

$$


where $n$ is a positive integer known as the principal quantum number. The corresponding eigenfunctions are given by:


$$

\psi_{nlm}(r,\theta,\phi) = R_{nl}(r)Y_{lm}(\theta,\phi)

$$


where $R_{nl}(r)$ is the radial wave function and $Y_{lm}(\theta,\phi)$ is the spherical harmonic function.



The hydrogen atom has played a crucial role in the development of quantum mechanics and has many practical applications, including in spectroscopy and the study of atomic and molecular structures.



In the next section, we will explore the quantum nature of light and matter in more detail and discuss the implications of the harmonic oscillator and hydrogen atom for our understanding of the quantum world.





# Fundamentals of Photonics: Quantum Electronics



## Chapter 4: Quantum Nature of Light and Matter



### Section: 4.3 Harmonic Oscillator and Hydrogen Atom



### Subsection: 4.3b Quantum Harmonic Oscillator and Energy Levels



In the previous subsection, we introduced the concept of the quantum harmonic oscillator and its importance in understanding the behavior of atoms, molecules, and other systems in quantum mechanics. In this subsection, we will delve deeper into the energy levels of the quantum harmonic oscillator and explore the factorization method for solving the Schrödinger equation.



#### Energy Levels of the Quantum Harmonic Oscillator



As mentioned in the previous subsection, the energy eigenvalues of the quantum harmonic oscillator are given by:


$$

E_n = \left(n+\frac{1}{2}\right)\hbar\omega

$$


where $n$ is a non-negative integer. This means that the energy levels of the quantum harmonic oscillator are equally spaced, with a difference of $\hbar\omega$ between each level. This is in contrast to the classical harmonic oscillator, where the energy levels are continuous.



#### Factorization Method



To solve the Schrödinger equation for the quantum harmonic oscillator, we can use the factorization method. This method involves finding a suitable factorization of the Hamiltonian operator, which can then be used to solve for the energy eigenvalues and eigenfunctions.



A suitable factorization for the quantum harmonic oscillator is given by:


$$

C_l = p_r + \frac{i\hbar(l+1)}{r} - i\mu \omega r

$$


with


$$

F_l = -(2l+3)\mu \omega \hbar

$$


and


$$

G_l = -(2l+1)\mu \omega \hbar

$$


Using this factorization, we can solve for the energy eigenvalues and eigenfunctions of the quantum harmonic oscillator. The energy eigenvalues are given by:


$$

E_{l+1}^{n'} = E_l^n + \frac{F_l - G_l}{2\mu} = E_l^n - \omega \hbar

$$


and continuing this, we get:


$$

E_{l+2}^{n'} = E_l^n - 2\omega \hbar \\

E_{l+3}^{n'} = E_l^n - 3\omega \hbar \\

\dots

$$


This means that the energy levels of the quantum harmonic oscillator decrease by $\omega\hbar$ with each successive level.



#### Termination of Energy Levels



One interesting aspect of the quantum harmonic oscillator is that the energy levels must terminate at some point. This can be seen from the fact that the Hamiltonian only has positive energy levels, as shown by the following equation:


$$

\langle \psi|2\mu H_l|\psi\rangle = \langle \psi|C_l^*C_l|\psi\rangle + \langle \psi|(2l+3)\mu \omega \hbar|\psi\rangle

$$


This means that for some value of $l$, the series must terminate with:


$$

C_{l_{max}} |nl_{max}\rangle = 0

$$


and then:


$$

E^n_{l_{max}} = -F_{l_{max}}/ (2 \mu) = (l_{max} + 3/2) \omega\hbar

$$


This is the lowest possible energy level for the quantum harmonic oscillator, and it is decreasing in energy by $\omega\hbar$ unless for some value of $l$, $C_l|nl\rangle = 0$. Identifying this value as $n$ gives:


$$

E_l^n = -F_l = (n + 3/2) \omega \hbar

$$


#### Recursion Relation and Degeneracy



Using the factorization method, we can also find a recursion relation for the quantum harmonic oscillator. This relation involves the lowering operator $C^*$ and the eigenvalue $\lambda$ and has the solution:


$$

\lambda^n_l = -\mu\omega\hbar\sqrt{2(n-l)}

$$


This recursion relation also reveals the degeneracy of the quantum harmonic oscillator. In addition to the degeneracy caused by angular momentum, there is also additional degeneracy caused by the oscillator potential. This can be seen by considering the states:


$$

|n \, n\rangle, |n-1 \, n-1\rangle, |n-2 \, n-2\rangle, \dots

$$


and applying the lowering operators $C^*$:


$$

C^*_{n-2}|n-1 \, n-1\rangle, C^*_{n-4}C^*_{n-3}|n-2 \, n-2\rangle, \dots

$$


This reveals that there is a degeneracy of $n$ for each energy level, caused by the oscillator potential. This degeneracy is important in understanding the behavior of atoms and molecules, as it can lead to the splitting of spectral lines in spectroscopy.



In conclusion, the quantum harmonic oscillator is an important model in understanding the quantum nature of light and matter. Its energy levels and degeneracy play a crucial role in many applications in physics, making it a fundamental concept in the study of photonics and quantum electronics.





# Fundamentals of Photonics: Quantum Electronics



## Chapter 4: Quantum Nature of Light and Matter



### Section: 4.3 Harmonic Oscillator and Hydrogen Atom



### Subsection: 4.3c Hydrogen Atom and Atomic Orbitals



In the previous subsection, we discussed the quantum harmonic oscillator and its energy levels. In this subsection, we will apply this knowledge to the hydrogen atom and explore the concept of atomic orbitals.



#### Hydrogen Atom and Energy Levels



The hydrogen atom is a simple system consisting of a single proton and a single electron. In the Bohr model of the atom, the electron orbits the proton in circular orbits, with the energy levels given by:


$$

E_n = -\frac{me^4}{8\epsilon_0^2h^2n^2}

$$


where $m$ is the mass of the electron, $e$ is the charge of the electron, $\epsilon_0$ is the permittivity of free space, and $h$ is Planck's constant. This equation is similar to the energy levels of the quantum harmonic oscillator, with the only difference being the negative sign.



#### Atomic Orbitals



In quantum mechanics, the electron in the hydrogen atom is described by a wave function, which gives the probability of finding the electron at a certain position. The solutions to the Schrödinger equation for the hydrogen atom result in a set of wave functions, known as atomic orbitals.



The first three atomic orbitals are the s, p, and d orbitals, which correspond to the quantum numbers $l=0,1,2$ respectively. These orbitals have different shapes and orientations, and each can hold a maximum of 2, 6, and 10 electrons respectively.



The s orbital is spherical in shape and has no angular nodes, meaning that the probability of finding the electron is highest at the center of the orbital. The p orbital, on the other hand, has a dumbbell shape and has one angular node, resulting in two lobes with opposite phases. The d orbital has a more complex shape, with four lobes and two angular nodes.



#### Electron Configurations and the Periodic Table



The electron configurations of elements can be determined by filling up the atomic orbitals in a specific order. The Aufbau principle states that electrons will fill the lowest energy orbitals first, and the Pauli exclusion principle states that each orbital can hold a maximum of two electrons with opposite spins.



The periodic table is organized based on the electron configurations of elements. The rows correspond to the principal quantum number $n$, while the columns correspond to the number of electrons in the outermost energy level, known as the valence electrons. The periodic table can also be used to predict the electron configurations of elements beyond the known elements, as shown in the related context.



#### Table of Spherical Harmonics



The atomic orbitals can also be described using spherical harmonics, which are solutions to the angular part of the Schrödinger equation. These spherical harmonics have corresponding atomic orbital symbols, as shown in the related context.



The s orbital corresponds to the spherical harmonic $Y_{00}$, the p orbital corresponds to the spherical harmonics $Y_{1m}$, the d orbital corresponds to the spherical harmonics $Y_{2m}$, and so on. This notation is useful in understanding the shapes and orientations of the atomic orbitals.



In the next subsection, we will explore the concept of spin and its role in the hydrogen atom and other systems.





# Fundamentals of Photonics: Quantum Electronics



## Chapter 4: Quantum Nature of Light and Matter



### Section: 4.4 Wave Mechanics



### Subsection: 4.4a Introduction to Wave Mechanics



In the previous section, we discussed the quantum nature of light and matter, and how it is described by wave-particle duality. In this section, we will delve deeper into the wave aspect of quantum mechanics and explore the concept of wave mechanics.



#### The Wave Equation



The wave equation is a fundamental equation in physics that describes the behavior of waves. It is a second-order partial differential equation that relates the second derivative of a wave function to its spatial and temporal derivatives. In the context of quantum mechanics, the wave equation is used to describe the behavior of particles, such as electrons, in a quantum system.



#### Investigation by Numerical Methods



One way to understand the wave equation is through numerical methods. By approximating a continuous string with a finite number of equidistant mass points, we can create a physical model that represents the behavior of the string. This model takes into account factors such as mass, tension, and separation between the mass points.



#### Discrete Formulation of the Wave Equation



In this discrete formulation, the vertical motion of each mass point is described by the following equation:


$$

\ddot u_i = \left(\frac{f}{\rho(\Delta x)^2}\right) (u_{i+1} + u_{i-1} - 2u_i)

$$


where $\ddot u_i$ is the second derivative of the vertical position of the $i$th mass point, $f$ is the tension of the string, $\rho$ is the mass density, and $\Delta x$ is the separation between the mass points.



#### Wave Equation in Continuous Form



In the continuous form, the wave equation takes the form:


$$

\frac{\partial^2 u}{\partial t^2} = c^2 \frac{\partial^2 u}{\partial x^2}

$$


where $u$ is a continuous function of two variables, $t$ and $x$, and $c$ is the speed of the wave.



#### Boundary Conditions



In order to solve the wave equation, we must also consider boundary conditions. For a string with fixed endpoints, the boundary conditions are given by:


$$

u(0,t) = u(L,t) = 0

$$


where $L$ is the length of the string. These boundary conditions ensure that the string remains fixed at its endpoints.



#### Application to Quantum Mechanics



The wave equation is a crucial tool in understanding the behavior of particles in quantum mechanics. By applying the principles of wave mechanics, we can better understand the behavior of particles, such as electrons, in a quantum system. In the next section, we will explore the application of wave mechanics to the hydrogen atom and atomic orbitals.





# Fundamentals of Photonics: Quantum Electronics



## Chapter 4: Quantum Nature of Light and Matter



### Section: 4.4 Wave Mechanics



### Subsection: 4.4b Wave Packets and Group Velocity



In the previous subsection, we discussed the basics of wave mechanics and the wave equation. In this subsection, we will explore the concept of wave packets and group velocity, and how they relate to the behavior of quantum particles.



#### Wave Packets



A wave packet is a localized wave that is composed of a superposition of many different waves with different wavelengths and frequencies. In other words, it is a combination of many plane waves with different amplitudes and phases. This concept is similar to the idea of a musical chord, which is composed of multiple individual notes with different frequencies and amplitudes.



In quantum mechanics, wave packets are used to describe the behavior of particles, such as electrons, in a quantum system. The wave function of a particle can be represented as a wave packet, with the amplitude of the wave packet representing the probability of finding the particle at a certain position.



#### Group Velocity



The group velocity of a wave packet is defined as the velocity at which the entire wave packet propagates. It is different from the phase velocity, which is the velocity at which the individual peaks within the wave packet move. The group velocity is given by the formula:


$$

v_g = \frac{\partial \omega}{\partial k}

$$


where $\omega$ is the angular frequency and $k$ is the wave vector. This formula is similar to the classical formula for velocity, $v = \frac{d}{dt}x$, where $x$ is the position and $t$ is time.



#### Relationship between Group Velocity and Phase Velocity



The group velocity and phase velocity are related by the dispersion relation, which describes how the frequency of a wave depends on its wave vector. In the case of a free particle, the dispersion relation is given by:


$$

\omega(k) = \frac{\hbar k^2}{2m}

$$


where $\hbar$ is the reduced Planck's constant and $m$ is the mass of the particle. Using this dispersion relation, we can see that the group velocity and phase velocity are equal, as the derivative of $\omega$ with respect to $k$ is simply $\frac{\hbar k}{m}$, which is the same as the classical velocity of the particle.



#### Spread of the Wave Packet



The concept of group velocity is based on a linear approximation to the dispersion relation near a particular value of $k$. However, for large times, this approximation fails to capture certain interesting aspects of the evolution of a free quantum particle. One such phenomenon is the spread of the wave packet, where the width of the wave packet, as measured by the uncertainty in position, grows linearly in time.



This spread of the wave packet is a result of the wave packet being composed of multiple waves with different frequencies and wavelengths. As the wave packet propagates, these waves interfere with each other, causing the wave packet to spread out. This phenomenon is an important aspect of quantum mechanics and is crucial in understanding the behavior of particles at the quantum level.



#### Conclusion



In this subsection, we explored the concept of wave packets and group velocity in the context of quantum mechanics. We saw how wave packets are used to describe the behavior of particles and how the group velocity and phase velocity are related. We also discussed the spread of the wave packet and its significance in quantum mechanics. In the next subsection, we will delve deeper into the behavior of free quantum particles and discuss the concept of wave-particle duality.





# Fundamentals of Photonics: Quantum Electronics



## Chapter 4: Quantum Nature of Light and Matter



### Section: 4.4 Wave Mechanics



### Subsection: 4.4c Scattering and Wavefunction Collapse



In the previous subsection, we discussed the concept of wave packets and group velocity in the context of quantum mechanics. In this subsection, we will explore the phenomenon of scattering and its relationship to wavefunction collapse.



#### Scattering



Scattering is a fundamental process in quantum mechanics where a particle interacts with a potential and changes its trajectory. This can occur when a particle encounters an obstacle or when it interacts with another particle. The scattering process can be described using the Schrödinger equation, which governs the evolution of the wave function of a quantum system.



In the case of a single particle scattering off a potential, the Schrödinger equation can be written as:


$$

i\hbar \frac{\partial \psi}{\partial t} = \hat{H}\psi

$$


where $\psi$ is the wave function, $\hat{H}$ is the Hamiltonian operator, and $\hbar$ is the reduced Planck's constant. The Hamiltonian operator can be written as the sum of the kinetic energy operator and the potential energy operator, $\hat{H} = \hat{T} + \hat{V}$.



When a particle interacts with a potential, its wave function is modified and the particle's trajectory is altered. This can be seen as a scattering event, where the particle's initial wave function is scattered into a new wave function. The probability of finding the particle at a certain position after the scattering event is given by the squared magnitude of the wave function, $|\psi|^2$.



#### Wavefunction Collapse



In quantum mechanics, the wave function of a particle is described as a superposition of all possible states that the particle can be in. However, when a measurement is made on the particle, the wave function collapses into a single state. This phenomenon is known as wavefunction collapse.



The collapse of the wave function can be seen as a result of the interaction between the particle and the measuring device. The measurement process causes the particle to interact with the potential of the measuring device, which alters the particle's wave function and collapses it into a single state.



#### Relationship between Scattering and Wavefunction Collapse



The scattering process and wavefunction collapse are closely related in quantum mechanics. Both involve the interaction of a particle with a potential, resulting in a modification of the particle's wave function. In the case of scattering, the particle's wave function is scattered into a new state, while in the case of wavefunction collapse, the wave function collapses into a single state.



Furthermore, the concept of wave packets and group velocity can also be applied to the scattering process. Just as a wave packet can be described as a superposition of many different waves, the scattered wave function can be seen as a superposition of different states. The group velocity of the scattered wave function can also be calculated using the same formula as before, with the wave vector representing the momentum of the scattered particle.



In conclusion, the concepts of scattering and wavefunction collapse are important in understanding the behavior of quantum particles. They demonstrate the fundamental principles of quantum mechanics and the role of interactions in shaping the evolution of a quantum system. 





# Fundamentals of Photonics: Quantum Electronics



## Chapter 4: Quantum Nature of Light and Matter



### Section: 4.5 Dirac Formalism and Matrix Mechanics



### Subsection: 4.5a Introduction to Dirac Formalism and Matrix Mechanics



In the previous section, we discussed the concept of wave mechanics and its application to scattering and wavefunction collapse. In this section, we will introduce the Dirac formalism and matrix mechanics, which provide a more elegant and powerful framework for understanding quantum mechanics.



#### Dirac Algebra



The Dirac formalism is based on the use of matrices to represent quantum mechanical operators. This approach was first introduced by Paul Dirac in 1925 and has since become an essential tool in the study of quantum mechanics.



One of the key components of the Dirac formalism is the use of the Lorentz algebra, which describes the symmetries of space and time. The Lorentz algebra is represented by the spin group <math>\text{Spin}(1,3)</math>, which is connected to the Lorentz group <math>\text{SO}(1,3)</math>. The generators of this representation are denoted by <math>S^{\mu\nu}</math>, which are matrices that satisfy the Lorentz algebra.



#### Spinors and Lorentz Transformations



In the Dirac formalism, spinors are used to represent quantum states. These are elements of the vector space <math>\mathbb{C}^4</math> and are denoted by <math>\psi</math>. The action of the spin generators <math>S^{\mu\nu}</math> on a spinor <math>\psi</math> corresponds to an infinitesimal Lorentz transformation.



To describe a finite Lorentz transformation, we can use the components <math>\omega_{\mu\nu}</math> (antisymmetric in <math>\mu,\nu</math>) to construct the transformation matrix <math>S</math>. This matrix satisfies the property that <math>S^{\mu\nu} = \omega^{\mu\nu} + \mathcal{O}(\omega^2)</math>, which allows us to express the finite Lorentz transformation as <math>\psi' = S\psi</math>.



#### Dirac Adjoint and Lorentz Invariant Quantities



To construct Lorentz invariant quantities, we need to define the Dirac adjoint for spinors <math>\psi</math>. This is denoted by <math>\bar{\psi}</math> and is defined as <math>\bar{\psi} = \psi^\dagger \gamma^0</math>, where <math>\gamma^0</math> is the time component of the Dirac gamma matrices.



Using the Dirac adjoint, we can construct Lorentz invariant quantities such as the Dirac Lagrangian. This is given by <math>\mathcal{L} = \bar{\psi}(i\gamma^\mu\partial_\mu - m)\psi</math>, where <math>m</math> is the mass of the particle.



#### Quartic Power and γ<sup>5</sup>



In the previous section, we discussed the concept of wave packets and their relationship to scattering. In the Dirac formalism, we can also consider the quartic subspace, which contains a single basis element denoted by <math>\epsilon_{\mu\nu\rho\sigma}</math>. This element is antisymmetric under exchange of any two adjacent gamma matrices.



In the context of the complex span, this basis element can also be written as <math>\gamma^5 = i\gamma^0\gamma^1\gamma^2\gamma^3</math>. This is known as the chirality operator and plays an important role in the study of quantum mechanics.



#### Conclusion



In this subsection, we have introduced the Dirac formalism and matrix mechanics, which provide a powerful framework for understanding quantum mechanics. We have discussed the use of spinors to represent quantum states and the role of the Lorentz algebra in this formalism. We have also explored the concept of the Dirac adjoint and its application in constructing Lorentz invariant quantities. In the next subsection, we will delve deeper into the mathematical foundations of the Dirac formalism and its applications in quantum mechanics.





# Fundamentals of Photonics: Quantum Electronics



## Chapter 4: Quantum Nature of Light and Matter



### Section: 4.5 Dirac Formalism and Matrix Mechanics



### Subsection: 4.5b Dirac Notation and Bra-Ket Notation



In the previous section, we introduced the Dirac formalism and its use of matrices to represent quantum mechanical operators. In this section, we will delve deeper into the formalism and discuss the use of Dirac notation and bra-ket notation.



#### Dirac Notation



Dirac notation, also known as bra-ket notation, is a powerful tool for representing quantum states and operators. It uses angle brackets, <char|<math>\langle</math» and <char|<math>\rangle</math», and a vertical bar <char|<math>|</math», to construct "bras" and "kets".



A ket is of the form <math>|v \rangle</math>. Mathematically, it represents a vector, <math>\boldsymbol v</math>, in an abstract (complex) vector space <math>V</math>. In quantum mechanics, a ket represents a state of a quantum system.



A bra is of the form <math>\langle f|</math>. Mathematically, it represents a linear form <math>f:V \to \Complex</math>, which is a linear map that maps each vector in <math>V</math> to a number in the complex plane <math>\Complex</math>. In quantum mechanics, a bra represents a dual state to a ket.



#### Bra-Ket Notation



The notation <math>\langle f | v\rangle \in \Complex</math> represents the action of a linear functional <math>\langle f|</math> on a vector <math>|v\rangle</math>. In other words, it is the inner product of the bra <math>\langle f|</math> and the ket <math>|v\rangle</math>.



Assuming that the vector space <math>V</math> has an inner product <math>(\cdot,\cdot)</math> with antilinear first argument, we can identify each vector <math>\boldsymbol \phi \equiv |\phi\rangle</math> with a corresponding linear form by placing the vector in the anti-linear first slot of the inner product: <math>(\boldsymbol\phi,\cdot) \equiv \langle\phi|</math>. This correspondence allows us to express the inner product between two vectors <math>\boldsymbol\phi</math> and <math>\boldsymbol\psi</math> as <math>(\boldsymbol\phi, \boldsymbol\psi) \equiv \langle\phi|\psi\rangle</math>.



#### Properties of Bra-Ket Notation



Bra-ket notation has several useful properties that make it a powerful tool in quantum mechanics. Some of these properties include:



- Linearity: <math>\langle a\boldsymbol\phi + b\boldsymbol\psi | \boldsymbol\chi\rangle = a\langle\boldsymbol\phi|\boldsymbol\chi\rangle + b\langle\boldsymbol\psi|\boldsymbol\chi\rangle</math>

- Orthogonality: <math>\langle\boldsymbol\phi|\boldsymbol\psi\rangle = 0</math> if <math>\boldsymbol\phi</math> and <math>\boldsymbol\psi</math> are orthogonal

- Completeness: <math>\sum_i |\boldsymbol\phi_i\rangle\langle\boldsymbol\phi_i| = \mathbb{I}</math>, where <math>\mathbb{I}</math> is the identity operator



These properties make bra-ket notation a convenient and intuitive way to represent quantum states and operators.



#### Applications of Bra-Ket Notation



Bra-ket notation is widely used in quantum mechanics to represent various physical quantities and operations. For example, the expectation value of an observable <math>\hat{A}</math> in a state <math>|\psi\rangle</math> can be written as <math>\langle\psi|\hat{A}|\psi\rangle</math>. Similarly, the time evolution of a state <math>|\psi(t)\rangle</math> can be expressed as <math>|\psi(t)\rangle = e^{-i\hat{H}t/\hbar}|\psi(0)\rangle</math>, where <math>\hat{H}</math> is the Hamiltonian operator.



Bra-ket notation also allows for a more compact and elegant representation of quantum mechanical equations and concepts. For example, the Schrödinger equation can be written as <math>i\hbar\frac{\partial}{\partial t}|\psi(t)\rangle = \hat{H}|\psi(t)\rangle</math>, where <math>\hat{H}</math> is the Hamiltonian operator.



#### Conclusion



In this subsection, we have discussed the use of Dirac notation and bra-ket notation in the Dirac formalism. These notations provide a powerful and intuitive way to represent quantum states and operators, making them essential tools in the study of quantum mechanics. In the next subsection, we will explore the concept of the Dirac adjoint and its role in Lorentz invariant quantities.





# Fundamentals of Photonics: Quantum Electronics



## Chapter 4: Quantum Nature of Light and Matter



### Section: 4.5 Dirac Formalism and Matrix Mechanics



### Subsection: 4.5c Matrix Representation of Operators



In the previous section, we discussed the use of matrices to represent quantum mechanical operators in the Dirac formalism. In this section, we will explore the matrix representation of operators in more detail.



#### Matrix Representation of Operators



In the Dirac formalism, operators are represented by matrices. These matrices act on the vector space of quantum states, and their elements correspond to the coefficients of the linear combination of basis states. For example, the matrix representation of the position operator <math>\hat{x}</math> in the position basis <math>|x\rangle</math> is given by <math>\langle x|\hat{x}|x'\rangle = x\delta(x-x')</math>.



Similarly, the matrix representation of the momentum operator <math>\hat{p}</math> in the momentum basis <math>|p\rangle</math> is given by <math>\langle p|\hat{p}|p'\rangle = p\delta(p-p')</math>.



The matrix representation of an operator can also be obtained by using the eigenstates of the operator as a basis. For example, the matrix representation of the Hamiltonian operator <math>\hat{H}</math> in the energy eigenbasis <math>|E_n\rangle</math> is given by <math>\langle E_n|\hat{H}|E_m\rangle = E_n\delta_{nm}</math>.



#### Matrix Representation of Composite Operators



Composite operators, which are formed by combining two or more operators, can also be represented by matrices. The matrix representation of a composite operator is obtained by multiplying the matrices of the individual operators.



For example, the matrix representation of the composite operator <math>\hat{A}\hat{B}</math> is given by <math>\langle x|\hat{A}\hat{B}|x'\rangle = \langle x|\hat{A}|x''\rangle\langle x''|\hat{B}|x'\rangle</math>.



#### Matrix Representation of Time Evolution Operator



The time evolution operator <math>\hat{U}(t,t_0)</math>, which describes the evolution of a quantum state from time <math>t_0</math> to time <math>t</math>, can also be represented by a matrix. The matrix elements of the time evolution operator are given by <math>\langle x|\hat{U}(t,t_0)|x'\rangle = \langle x|\hat{U}(t,t_0)|x'\rangle</math>, where <math>|x\rangle</math> and <math>|x'\rangle</math> are position eigenstates.



#### Conclusion



In this section, we have discussed the matrix representation of operators in the Dirac formalism. This representation allows us to perform calculations and make predictions about quantum systems using linear algebra techniques. In the next section, we will explore the use of matrix mechanics in solving quantum mechanical problems.





# Fundamentals of Photonics: Quantum Electronics



## Chapter 4: Quantum Nature of Light and Matter



### Section: 4.6 Harmonic Oscillator Revisited



In the previous section, we discussed the use of the factorization method to solve the 3D isotropic harmonic oscillator. In this section, we will revisit the harmonic oscillator and explore its quantum nature in more detail.



#### Introduction to Harmonic Oscillator Revisited



The harmonic oscillator is a fundamental system in quantum mechanics that has a potential given by <math>V(r) = \mu \omega^2 r^2/2</math>. This potential can be managed using the factorization method, which involves finding suitable factorization of the Hamiltonian operator.



### Factorization Method



The factorization method involves finding suitable factorization of the Hamiltonian operator. In the case of the harmonic oscillator, a suitable factorization is given by <math>C_l = p_r + \frac{i\hbar(l+1)}{r} - i\mu \omega r</math>, with <math>F_l = -(2l+3)\mu \omega \hbar</math> and <math>G_l = -(2l+1)\mu \omega \hbar</math>. This factorization allows us to rewrite the Hamiltonian operator as <math>H_l = C_l^*C_l + F_l</math>.



Using this factorization, we can derive the energy levels of the harmonic oscillator. Starting from the ground state <math>|0\rangle</math>, we can apply the raising operator <math>C_l^*</math> to obtain the first excited state <math>|1\rangle</math>. Continuing this process, we can derive the energy levels as <math>E_l^n = -F_l = (n + 3/2) \omega \hbar</math>, where <math>n</math> is the number of excitations.



### Degeneracy in the Harmonic Oscillator



There are two sources of degeneracy in the harmonic oscillator: angular momentum and the oscillator potential. The states <math>|n \, n\rangle, |n-1 \, n-1\rangle, |n-2 \, n-2\rangle, \dots</math> have the same energy level, but different angular momentum quantum numbers. This degeneracy can be broken by applying the lowering operator <math>C^*</math>, which results in a recursion relation on <math>\lambda</math> with solution <math>\lambda^n_l = -\mu\omega\hbar\sqrt{2(n-l)}</math>.



The second source of degeneracy is caused by the oscillator potential. This degeneracy can be broken by identifying the value of <math>l</math> for which the series terminates, resulting in a unique energy level <math>E^n_{l_{\max}} = (l_{\max} + 3/2) \omega\hbar</math>. This energy level decreases by <math>\omega\hbar</math> for each additional excitation, unless <math>C_l|nl\rangle = 0</math>, in which case the energy level remains constant.



In conclusion, the harmonic oscillator is a fundamental system in quantum mechanics that exhibits interesting quantum properties such as degeneracy and energy quantization. The factorization method provides a powerful tool for solving the harmonic oscillator and understanding its quantum nature. 





# Fundamentals of Photonics: Quantum Electronics



## Chapter 4: Quantum Nature of Light and Matter



### Section: 4.6 Harmonic Oscillator Revisited



In the previous section, we discussed the use of the factorization method to solve the 3D isotropic harmonic oscillator. In this section, we will revisit the harmonic oscillator and explore its quantum nature in more detail.



#### Introduction to Harmonic Oscillator Revisited



The harmonic oscillator is a fundamental system in quantum mechanics that has a potential given by $V(r) = \mu \omega^2 r^2/2$. This potential can be managed using the factorization method, which involves finding suitable factorization of the Hamiltonian operator.



### Factorization Method



The factorization method involves finding suitable factorization of the Hamiltonian operator. In the case of the harmonic oscillator, a suitable factorization is given by $C_l = p_r + \frac{i\hbar(l+1)}{r} - i\mu \omega r$, with $F_l = -(2l+3)\mu \omega \hbar$ and $G_l = -(2l+1)\mu \omega \hbar$. This factorization allows us to rewrite the Hamiltonian operator as $H_l = C_l^*C_l + F_l$.



Using this factorization, we can derive the energy levels of the harmonic oscillator. Starting from the ground state $|0\rangle$, we can apply the raising operator $C_l^*$ to obtain the first excited state $|1\rangle$. Continuing this process, we can derive the energy levels as $E_l^n = -F_l = (n + 3/2) \omega \hbar$, where $n$ is the number of excitations.



### Degeneracy in the Harmonic Oscillator



There are two sources of degeneracy in the harmonic oscillator: angular momentum and the oscillator potential. The states $|n \, n\rangle, |n-1 \, n-1\rangle, |n-2 \, n-2\rangle, \dots$ have the same energy level, but different angular momentum quantum numbers. This degeneracy can be broken by applying the lowering operator $C^*$, which results in a reduction of the angular momentum by one unit. This process can be repeated to obtain all the possible states with the same energy level.



Another source of degeneracy is the oscillator potential. As we have seen, the energy levels of the harmonic oscillator are given by $E_l^n = (n + 3/2) \omega \hbar$. This means that for a given energy level, there are multiple states with different values of $n$. This degeneracy can be broken by applying the raising or lowering operators, which change the number of excitations and therefore, the energy level of the state.



### Coherent States and Squeezed States



In addition to the energy levels, the harmonic oscillator also has a set of coherent states and squeezed states. Coherent states are states that have a well-defined phase and amplitude, and they are eigenstates of the annihilation operator $a$. These states are given by $|\alpha\rangle = e^{-|\alpha|^2/2} \sum_{n=0}^{\infty} \frac{\alpha^n}{\sqrt{n!}} |n\rangle$, where $\alpha$ is a complex number.



Squeezed states, on the other hand, are states that have a reduced uncertainty in one of the quadratures of the harmonic oscillator. These states are obtained by applying a squeezing operator to the coherent states. The squeezing operator is given by $S(z) = e^{(z^*a^2 - za^{\dagger 2})/2}$, where $z$ is a complex number. The squeezed states are then given by $|z\rangle = S(z)|\alpha\rangle$.



Coherent states and squeezed states have important applications in quantum optics, as they can be used to describe the behavior of light in optical systems. They also have interesting properties, such as sub-Poissonian photon statistics and non-classical correlations, which make them useful for quantum information processing and quantum communication. In the next section, we will explore these applications in more detail.





# Fundamentals of Photonics: Quantum Electronics



## Chapter 4: Quantum Nature of Light and Matter



### Section: 4.6 Harmonic Oscillator Revisited



In the previous section, we discussed the use of the factorization method to solve the 3D isotropic harmonic oscillator. In this section, we will revisit the harmonic oscillator and explore its quantum nature in more detail.



#### Introduction to Harmonic Oscillator Revisited



The harmonic oscillator is a fundamental system in quantum mechanics that has a potential given by $V(r) = \mu \omega^2 r^2/2$. This potential can be managed using the factorization method, which involves finding suitable factorization of the Hamiltonian operator.



### Factorization Method



The factorization method involves finding suitable factorization of the Hamiltonian operator. In the case of the harmonic oscillator, a suitable factorization is given by $C_l = p_r + \frac{i\hbar(l+1)}{r} - i\mu \omega r$, with $F_l = -(2l+3)\mu \omega \hbar$ and $G_l = -(2l+1)\mu \omega \hbar$. This factorization allows us to rewrite the Hamiltonian operator as $H_l = C_l^*C_l + F_l$.



Using this factorization, we can derive the energy levels of the harmonic oscillator. Starting from the ground state $|0\rangle$, we can apply the raising operator $C_l^*$ to obtain the first excited state $|1\rangle$. Continuing this process, we can derive the energy levels as $E_l^n = -F_l = (n + 3/2) \omega \hbar$, where $n$ is the number of excitations.



### Degeneracy in the Harmonic Oscillator



There are two sources of degeneracy in the harmonic oscillator: angular momentum and the oscillator potential. The states $|n \, n\rangle, |n-1 \, n-1\rangle, |n-2 \, n-2\rangle, \dots$ have the same energy level, but different angular momentum quantum numbers. This degeneracy can be broken by applying the lowering operator $C^*$, which results in a reduction of the angular momentum by one unit. This process can be repeated to obtain all the possible states with the same energy level.



#### Quantum Optics and Harmonic Oscillator



The harmonic oscillator has important applications in quantum optics. In particular, it is used in the generation of single photons, correlated photon pairs, squeezed light, and entangled photons. This is possible due to the quantum nature of the harmonic oscillator, which allows for the manipulation and control of individual photons.



One example of this is the use of the harmonic oscillator in parametric amplifiers and oscillators. Unlike most typical parametric oscillators that use the second-order nonlinearity, those based on four-wave mixing use the third-order nonlinearity. This allows for the amplification and generation of squeezed light, which is useful for studying how atoms interact with squeezed light.



### Quantum Radar



Another application of the harmonic oscillator in quantum optics is in quantum radar. This technology has the potential to revolutionize radar systems by using quantum illumination to detect objects with low reflectivity or that are hidden behind obstacles. However, there are several challenges and limitations in implementing a truly-quantum radar prototype.



One of the main challenges is managing the idler pulse, which ideally should be jointly detected with the signal pulse returning from the target. This requires a quantum memory with a long coherence time, which is currently difficult to achieve. Other solutions, such as storing the idler pulse in a delay line using a standard optical fiber, can degrade the quantum correlations between the signal and idler pulses, reducing the quantum advantage of the system.



### Conclusion



In this section, we have revisited the harmonic oscillator and explored its quantum nature. We have seen how the factorization method can be used to derive the energy levels of the harmonic oscillator and how it has important applications in quantum optics, such as in the generation of single photons and in quantum radar. The harmonic oscillator continues to be a fundamental system in quantum mechanics and its study has led to many advancements in the field of photonics.





### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the quantum nature of light and matter, which is essential in understanding the fundamentals of photonics. We have learned that light can exhibit both wave-like and particle-like behavior, known as wave-particle duality. This duality is described by the famous equation $E=hf$, where $E$ is the energy of a photon, $h$ is Planck's constant, and $f$ is the frequency of the light. We have also discussed the concept of quantization, where energy and other physical quantities can only exist in discrete values, rather than continuous values.



Furthermore, we have delved into the concept of photons, which are the fundamental particles of light. We have seen that photons have properties such as energy, momentum, and spin, which can be described using quantum mechanics. We have also explored the concept of wavefunction, which describes the probability of finding a particle at a particular location. This concept is crucial in understanding the behavior of matter at the quantum level.



Finally, we have discussed the dual nature of matter, where particles such as electrons can also exhibit wave-like behavior. This duality is described by the de Broglie equation, $p=\frac{h}{\lambda}$, where $p$ is the momentum of the particle, $h$ is Planck's constant, and $\lambda$ is the wavelength of the particle. This concept is essential in understanding the behavior of electrons in electronic devices, which are crucial in modern photonics.



In conclusion, the quantum nature of light and matter is a fundamental aspect of photonics that allows us to understand and manipulate light at the smallest scales. It is a complex and fascinating field that continues to drive advancements in technology and our understanding of the universe.
